1586487981|5.0|Although I am an economist by training and have studied economics for many years, I admit that in reading this book I have learned a great deal about the complexities of both the theory and the practice of anti-poverty policies in developing nations.    Why are people so interested in the issue of global poverty?  Well, to list a few of the many aspects about poverty addressed in this book, every year about 9 million children die before they reach their fifth birthday, usually in the poorest countries.  In the developed world, a woman has a one-in-5,000 chance of dying while giving birth, but in many sub-Saharan Africa countries the odds are one-in-30.  There are at least 25 countries in the world with life expectancies of 55 years or less.  If these sorts of situations capture your mind and lead it to ask what can be done, one of the first things you might consider doing is learning more about the conditions and circumstances that lead to these revealing statistics.  That's where this book comes in.    So, is this book one you should buy?  Presumably that's why you are reading this.  Here are a few observations that may help you decide whether to buy this fine book:  In the authors' own words, the book \\"is ultimately about what the lives and choices of the poor tell us about how to fight global poverty.\\"  That may not sound too sexy or exciting, but if you have an interest in facts, theories and observations about global poverty, then this is your book.  On the other hand, if what you seek are simple theories and, especially, strong advocacy of a few preferred solutions, then you are probably barking up the wrong tree.  Don't get me wrong; I like the book just as it is.  There is so much information to consider and so many approaches to fighting poverty to contemplate.  Just don't expect the authors to take a lot of your time championing pet solutions.  Because the problem of poverty is itself rather complex, so are some of its solutions.  Jack Webb (the \\"just thefacts, ma'am\\" star of the \\"Dragnet\\" series) might have loved this fact-filled book.  At least, he'd love it if he was an economist or someone interested in learning (a lot) about global poverty.  Yet there's much more to the book that mere facts.  Primarily, there is a pursuit of understanding the circumstances associated with poverty and the efforts to overcome it.  That's where this book excels.    It's certainly early to judge, but this book could prove to be a classic in its field.  It successfully challenges and encourages the reader to think in new ways about anti-poverty initiatives.  Although its authors are probably unknown to the general public, they are well regarded in economics.  They both have received a number of prestigious awards, including the John Bates Clark Medal (to Esther Duflo) for the best American economist under age 40.  Previous winners of this award include a Who's Who of economists, such as Paul Samuelson, Milton Friedman, James Tobin, Kenneth Arrow, Gary Becker, Martin Feldstein, Lawrence Summers and Steven Levitt.    In short, this is a substantial book with a great deal of important content.  There are some graphs, but less than you might expect from two economists.  Importantly, it is readable and understandable by the interested lay reader.  Frankly, I think it's a book you won't forget.  If the issues of global poverty and economic development interest you, this is a book well worth your careful consideration.

0439023521|5.0|The Hunger Games opens in an unrecognizable America.  Years of devastating natural disasters have ravaged the land and left its people terrified and fighting for survival.  From that desperate chaos emerges a new government in which safety is offered in return for total submission.  Citizens of the Capitol enjoy the power and luxury of the ruling class, while inhabitants of the twelve districts under their dominion toil on the brink of starvation without complaint (if they know what's good for them).  The Capitol demands tribute from each district as penance for a past revolution attempt:  One boy and one girl to be thrown into an arena for a fight to the death, televised for national entertainment.  Sixteen-year-old Katniss Everdeen of District 12 finds herself an unlucky tribute.  We follow her as she comes to grips with the terrible unfairness of her situation and decides if and how she should fight back.    As a social studies teacher, my mind was whirring with the possible applications of this text in my classroom (although I'd like to note that the novel is an exciting read regardless of occupation or age--I read the entire series in a weekend, unable to put it down!).  The themes explored in this book align perfectly with the Participation in Government curriculum of New York State, and I'm sure the same is true of other curricula and other states.      It is normal for American youths to take their government for granted, to feel entitled to their freedoms and opportunities without examining the whys and hows of their privilege (and this goes for adults too!).  In order to encourage participation in government, students must examine government's purpose, origins, and operation.  I am frequently met with apathy and complacency on this front.  Concepts that are difficult for adolescents to grasp--ideas like Rousseau's social contract, Locke's natural rights, and the consent of the governed--can seem intangible and insignificant to the American teenager.  Katniss's fictional story offers adolescents a window into a world that is very real for people in other nations today.  When students view the concepts above through Katniss's eyes, engrossed in her heart-pounding struggle for survival, they become real and significant.  Students will feel Katniss's despair at being a second-class citizen.  They will share her angry and righteous desire to bring about the destruction of the oppressive Capitol.  And, with teacher guidance, they will expand their thinking about Katniss's government to think about their own world.    The Hunger Games is a novel that enthralls readers as much as any blockbuster action movie, but it also offers an opportunity to provoke serious and impassioned academic discussion.  Due to its broad appeal and its sophisticated themes, it is a must-read for any government class.

Almost every line is played for laughs. When it's not, it's setting up a laugh. Which is not to say that it's not a 'true' memoir - every chapter is oriented around a life experience or theme (not in an absolutely linear way, but close.) But the insights, even the deep ones - and there are some - are communicated in a laugh-out-loud, Tina Fey kind of way. Personally I loved it, and read it in a day.<br /><br />Other reviewers have mentioned a certain distance, and I think that is true - I think it's a product of her kind of humor. It requires a distance, a dispassionate eye. But this did not bother me in the slightest - her 'job' is to make us laugh, and she did that. And I respect her right to NOT bare her soul (or anything else) to the public (and I can think of more than a few other celebrities that might want to consider trying it...) 0525951652|2.0|This was one of those books I caught sight of and thought \\"Wow, Pillars of the Earth goes to the 20th Century!\\" and grabbed it, only to be disappointed.    Some have critiqued the shallowness of the characters and the unlikely coincidences--to some degree coincidences didn't bother me as I knew it would be a sweeping epic, where the characters kept running into each other in unlikely ways.    The shallowness of some of the characters, and most particularly, the horrible history was what turned me off.  Nearly every character who represents authority or privelige is, quite simply, a caricature of an upper class twit a la Monty Python, with few exceptions.  All the working class people are noble, knowledgable and articulate and all realize that their masters are idiots.  OK, I could handle that even though I found it a silly model for the book--and it didn't seem that was how the book was going to go, not for the first 100 pages or so.    The history is the biggest problem.  As a student of the era, I cringed when it became obvious that Follett was going to follow the \\"World War I disaster with mud, blood and poetry\\" model.  He uses his characters to present a shallow and frankly nonsensical model of how the war started.  Recent scholarship has quite clearly shown the role that Austria-Hungary and Germany played in turning a localized conflict into a world war.  All through the book, several characters bemoan how Germany \\"had no choice\\" but to go to war when Russian mobilized, a frankly silly notion.  Germany was the only nation that had a mobilization plan that involved violation of Belgium and Luxembourg as PART of mobilization--everyone else did not.      He almost goes so far as to indicate nobody was happy they had won in 1918--clearly silliness as any look at the historical record will indicate.  The demoralization came later and had much more to do with domestic politics, not with a perceived need to halt German aggression.    All British officers are idiots inthis book; in fact, all officers are with the exception of one of the younger German characters and the American advisor to President Wilson.  Wilson is portrayed as an almost god-like figure who knows what is best for all, and if only the benighted Europeans would just listen, all would be well. Wilson's inflexibility, stubborness and ability to offend his political equals is nowhere to be seen--the League of Nations fails \\"because everybody is stupid,\\" apparently.    While I expect some of the characters to believe such things, their introspective thoughts and some events are misleadingly portrayed as representing historical fact.  The socialist/working class point of view is portrayed as good, other views are silly and dangerous.      Political events in Russia are particularly misportrayed.  All the characters that oppose the Bolsheviks are idiots (or even perverts), while the Bolsheviks (who had to seize power in a coup) are seen as enjoying widespread support from everybody.  If things had been so simple, the Civil War would have been over in a week.  Allied intervention in Russia, which occured with cabinet support (UK) and the US administration, is depicted as a sneaky scheme engineered by Winston Churchill and one of the upper class twit characters.  Nor was the intervention \\"secret.\\"  Over time public support waned, but not at first.    Maybe this is the problem with trying to write a massive, sweeping epic, but I was disappointed and found it hard to stay engaged with the characters toward the end.  The bad history greatly diminished what started out as a very promising story.  I fear how the run up to World War II in the 20s and 30s will be portrayed in the next volume.

B003UESJH4|5.0|THE KING'S SPEECH is everything that a great movie should be.<br /><br />It tells a good story and it tells it well.<br /><br />After the death of his father (Michael Gambon) and the scandalous abdication of his older brother (Guy Pearce), Bertie (Colin Firth), who has suffered from a debilitating speech impediment all his life, is suddenly crowned King George VI of England.<br /><br />With the country on the brink of war, his wife (Helena Bonham Carter) arranges for her husband to see an eccentric speech therapist, Lionel Logue (Geoffrey Rush), and after a rough start, the two delve into an unorthodox course of treatment that will cure the monarch's stammer and help him find the voice to encourage his people.<br /><br />David Seidler's well-crafted screenplay and Tom Hooper's sensitive direction transports us back to a not-so-long-ago era of innocence when the world was about to be shattered, once again, by war.<br /><br />The film, which covers two decades, spends much of its time dealing with court intrigues, primarily involving the affair between Edward VIII (Pearce) and his affair with twice-divorced Wallis Simpson.<br /><br />However, the spine of this story is the relationship and growing friendship between two men, the future king (Firth) and a commoner (Rush), who helps the ruler overcome his disability and become the inspiration of his nation.<br /><br />The performances by the entire cast, including Timothy Spall (as Churchill), Derek Jacobi, Claire Bloom and Anthony Andrews are perfect.  They transform names from out history books into real, multi-dimensional people.<br /><br />Some writers have commented that THE KING'S SPEECH is historically inaccurate, in that it glosses over Edward's possible Nazi sympathies.<br /><br />Seidler's script touches briefly on that issue, but one must remember that this is not a documentary, but a dramatic presentation of actual events. B00475AYJQ|3.0|... or at least that's the impression you get from how quickly this book moves, leaping from plot point to plot point, quickly introducing and then resolving drama at the drop of a hat. There are several times throughout the story that Kvothe as the narrator literally just glosses over entire plot points because they \\"aren't important\\" or \\"they bore me\\"; he will literallt set up a fairly major problem or occurance with a lot of drama, and then the next page will have something to the effect of \\"... but suffice it to say that all turned out okay, and now let's pick up our narrative three weeks later\\". It feels out of place and rushed because of how much attention is lavished both at the beginning of the book and in its predecessor on the minutae of Kvothe's day-to-day life at the Arcanum. It makes me wonder if the author got halfway through the book, panicked a bit because he realised this is only a trilogy, and decided he had to skip a bunch of stuff he really wanted to (or should have)explored in earnest to balance out the narrative.     The relationship between Denna and Kvothe does feel as if it's being drug out far too long, with a frustrating amount of text given over to Kvothe sighing at the sight of her and endless flirtatious will-the-won't-they-he-wants-to-but-oh-she's-so-difficult-to-catch banter, but there are a few scenes between them that are absolutely beautiful. One, involving a moonlight stroll in a garden, is sweet and poignant and even a little heartbreaking in its intimacy, and I felt like the book desperately needed so much more of that. Not just with Denna, but with other characters, to give Kvothe a chance to act like a person instead of a bullet list of heroic, smarmy accomplishments. It would have made me like him more than I do, which unfortunately is not as much as I should for being the protagonist and narrator.    Kvothe, disappointingly, feels more like wish fulfillment than ever as his exploits grow. More than once I caught myself rolling my eyes at what was rapidly turning into a piece of Mary Stu fan-fiction. Kvothe learns a sacred warrior's way never before taught outside its culture. Kvothe becomes a sex god women fall before and spends a ridiculous amount of time spouting smarmy, snort-worthy tripe about how much more he understands the gender than anyone else. It's frustrating because the rest of the cast feels like they're only there to be slavishly devoted to him, act as straw-men for him to defeat, or react in awe to the things he does. Even Kvothe's friends, Wil and Sim, are characterised by little more than how much they're willing to do for him; neither of them get any significant development, and the only time they're around is when they're bending over backwards to save Kvothe from himself or discussing how brilliant something he did was, or bemoaning how unfortunate it is that they aren't as clever as he. It's like they cease to exist when they aren't helping him with something... certainly he never spares them a thought otherwise.    I realise that this is essentially a fictional autobiography, and if you enjoy that sort of thing you'll probably find the One Man Show vibe a lot more appealing than I do, since it's basically expected, but with the spotlight planted so firmly on Kvothe and building him up into a legendary figure, there isn't a chance for the rest of the world to grow and really feel like somewhere you want to return to, with characters you want to know better.    There were things I liked about the book, of course; the dialogue is snappy, and at times laugh out loud funny. The environments and different cultures are fascinating. But Wise Man's Fear really does feel like it suffers too much from Middle Child Syndrome. A lot happens in the book, and a lot of it is entertaining, but so little of it feels like it's important at all to the big plot hook set up in the original book; that of Kvothe's quest to find the Chandrian. It almost feels like half of it could have been released as a collection of short stories after the trilogy is done and been better for it, giving us more time to become invested in the dark magic and mystery of the quest. As it is, it's a bit like listening to a very self absorbed person tell a story at a bar, an endless parade of \\"So then I, I, I, I\\" that is entertaining, but ultimately not as meaningful or memorable as it could have been.

B000002U94|5.0|Detroit rocker Bob Seger and his Silver Bullet Band released its second live album entitled Nine Tonight in September of 1981.<br />Nine Tonight was Seger's thirteenth album in total. I think that this live album is just as good, if not better than the first live album, 1976's Live Bullet (which saw a young, hungry Seger prior to stardom whereas Nine Tonight captures him at the height of his success with the #1 album Against the Wind).<br />Half of Nine Tonight, like Live Bullet, was recorded at the legendary Cobo Hall in Detroit, MI (tracks 4, 7, 8, 10, 11, 12, 13 and 14) in June of 1980 and the album's other eight tracks (tracks 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, 9, 15 and 16) were recorded at the long demolished Boston Garden in my native Boston, MA back in October, 1980 and mixed at Bayshore Recording Studios in Florida by Bill Szymczyk (who produced albums for The Eagles, Joe Walsh, J Geils Band and The Who).<br />Seger is joined by longtime Silver Bullet members bass player Chris Campbell and sax player Alto Reed (both of whom are still in the band today) and guitarist Drew Abbott (whom would leave The Silver Bullet Band during The Distance sessions in 1982). There were some changes to The Silver Bullet Band lineup since Live Bullet. First, drummer Charlie Allen Martin was paralyzed in a car accident in 1977 and was replaced by David Teegarden (whom would also leave the band after Nine Tonight's release). Second, keyboard player Robyn Robbins was replaced in 1980 by ex-Grand Funk Railroad keyboard player Craig Frost (whom made his debut on Nine Tonight and is still in The Silver Bullet Band today).<br />The only two exclusive tracks on Nine Tonight is the opening title cut which was from the movie \\"Urban Cowboy\\" released in 1980. The track was intended for Against the Wind but the producer for the \\"Urban Cowboy\\" soundtrack album (Eagles manager Irving Azoff) had the song ready for inclusion on the album (UC). Then we segue into the album's biggest hit \\"Tryin' To Live My Life Without You\\" which is a great song.<br />Nine Tonight also features blistering live versions of tracks from Seger's three million sellers 1976's Night Moves (represented by \\"Mainstreet\\", \\"The Fire Down Below\\", the title cut to Night Moves and \\"Rock and Roll Never Forgets\\"), 1978's Stranger in Town (represented by \\"Hollywood Nights\\", \\"Old Time Rock and Roll\\", \\"Feel Like a Number\\" and \\"We've Got Tonight\\") and 1980's chart topper Against the Wind (represented by \\"You'll Accomp'ny Me', the album's title cut, his ode to Jane Fonda \\"Her Strut\\", \\"Fire Lake\\" and \\"Betty Lou's Gettin' Out Tonight\\").<br />We end the album, like Live Bullet, with \\"Let it Rock\\". This version is more electrifying than on the Live Bullet version clocking in at over 10 plus minutes. The CD however features an edited version because otherwise, it would have been believed back in 1989, that the track would exceed the maximum playing time on a CD or it would have become a 2 CD set (CDs can fit up to 80 minutes of music). The LP label gave Let it Rock's playing time as 8 minutes and 26 seconds but it is actually 10 minutes and 35 seconds. The CD version is only a little over 6 minutes and uses the ending of The Fire Down Below to end the track instead of the big finale so that's my only gripe about the CD (I hope a remastered CD is released soon with the full version like on the original LP, cassette and 8-track issues).<br />Nine Tonight was released to massive sales and the album peaked at #3 on the Billboard album charts and sold millions upon release. The album deserves to be re-released in its full glory and can fit on one CD no problem or worse comes to worse, re-release it on two (I'll be a first day buyer).<br />Highly recommended!ch is a great song. The CD version is only a little over 6 minutes and uses the ending of The Fire Down Below to end the track instead of the big finale so that's my only gripe about the CD (I hope a remastered CD is released soon with the full version like on the original LP, cassette and 8-track issues).   Nine Tonight was released to massive sales and the album peaked at #3 on the Billboard album charts and sold millions upon release. The album deserves to be re-released in its full glory and can fit on one CD no problem or worse comes to worse, re-release it on two (I'll be a first day buyer).   Highly recommended!
0670022411|5.0|While reading through A Discovery of Witches, I confess, I couldn't help but compare it to Twilight. That being said, most of those comparisons consisted of, \\"This is what Twilight should have been.\\" They share some of the same elements, chiefly a romance involving a powerful male vampire, but where Twilight stays firmly entrenched in the land of teenyboppers, this romance is of a more mature variety. If mature intelligent adults want to fantasize about a vampire romance that's not caught up in being dark, edgy, or 'big-city' modern, this is the way to go about it!<br /><br />Unlike the bulk of urban fantasy, the characters here are not hip, on the edge of fashion, incredibly popular, or filled with woes about their latest crush or how their job isn't fun. They're professionals, mostly researchers, and they come across as people who have made peace with themselves. Even the main character, Diana, who in many ways has not actually made peace with herself and her magical heritage, still understands who she is, is comfortable with herself as a person, and is refreshingly more focused on research papers than parties, scholarship more than socializing. She's the kind of character I look at and say to myself, \\"I want to be just like her,\\" and I can relate to her because she isn't what so many urban fantasy heroines are. She isn't young, pretty, and modern. She's dedicated, fairly quiet, and interested in academia.<br /><br />The research that the author put into this novel shows. Harkness has definitely done her homework when it comes to alchemy, genetics, location, culture, history, and, unsurprisingly, wine. While occasionally imparting all this information borders on breaking the \\"show, don't tell\\" rule of writing, honestly, I can't imagine another way that it could have all been presented in a way that makes sense both to the reader and in the way that the characters are explaining. Harkness evidently spent a good amount of time asking all the right questions (for example, if vampires have extra chromosomes, just how do they get them when they came from human stock?) and finding good answers to them that make sense. I learned a good deal while reading this, and moreover, I also experienced that mental shift that comes with asking your own questions and trying to figure out the answers about things you may never have considered before.<br /><br />Harkness weaves a wonderful mystery, dropping hints in all the right places and tying things together very smoothly. She can make things sexy without being graphic, and make academia sound far more exciting than it probably has any rights to be. (At least for those who aren't total bibliophiles, that is!) Her style is wonderful, flowing nicely along and carrying the reader across time and pages to a place where they won't want to leave. This is one author well worth keeping an eye on, and I'm very excited for the next book of the series. This comes highly recommended; do not miss your chance to read this one!s have extra chromosomes, just how do they get them when they came from human stock?) and finding good answers to them that make sense. Perhaps, some people need reminding of this.

This honestly reads like the first draft of a novel that was never finished and I can only assume that the publishers were desperate to get it on the shelves and so neglected to give the writer sufficient time to have it proof-read, sense-checked, edited and re-written into something coherent. B0001UL7RY|3.0|I find it humorous when I read the review I wrote for `Let Go' quite a while back.  In that review I mentioned how this album was a step up.  Well, upon a recent listen I've appended that statement since, well, it's not true.  As a whole `Under My Skin' is actually a step backwards from her initial album, serving up a lot more filler than necessary.  Thankfully she traded in some of her ways for a fresher sound (I still uphold that her third album is sublime) but `Under My Skin' frays towards the end to deliver an uneven album with a few goodies.<br /><br />So here it is, track for track.<br /><br />`Take Me Away' is a decent album opener, although it doesn't bring anything substantial to the table.  It showcases Avril's knack for pop/rock and the way she can nail a chorus and bring it all home, but there is something very dated about the tracks construction.  It doesn't age as well as some of her other tracks.  `Together' ages very well, standing up even today as one of her finer efforts.  The piano laden musical arrangement and Avril's vocals on the chorus are pitch-perfect.<br /><br />The angst-ridden `alternative-pop' scene does come over a tad `overdone' but remembering that this album was released during the genres heyday will help you appreciate what Avril attempts to accomplish here.<br /><br />`Don't Tell Me' was the album's first single, and it was a great choice.  I love the stripped down feel of the verses and then the `pure Avril' chorus kicks in with an added edge of earnestness.  Her vocals are distinct (like a younger, less refined Alanis Morissette) and they work marvelously here.  Sure, some of the lyrics seem simple and cheap, but she delivers them with a veneer of conviction that elevates the song.  It's still one of my favorite Avril songs.<br /><br />`He Wasn't' is one of the big issues I have with early Avril; she doesn't understand who she is as an artist.  The whole `let me pretend to be punk rock and release a vintage Green Dayesque record' just doesn't work with her.  The musical arrangement itself fees ripped straight from Green Day's `Insomniac' record.  `How Does It Feel' finds Avril trying to tap into the success of `I'm With You', slowing things down and trying to pull out a sincere and moving track that just doesn't do it.  It doesn't have the ghostly quality of `I'm With You', despite trying very hard to.  `My Happy Ending' was probably one of Avril's biggest hits; in fact they still play this one the radio often.  It is cheery in structure despite the downer-type message.  It is bouncy and catchy and fun.  `Nobody's Home' captures some of the eerie sincerity that `How Does It Feel' misses.  Sure, it isn't exactly the same breed of track, but it conveys the feelings that `How Does It Feel' couldn't.  Maybe that's because Avril vocals are better suited for `big' things.  She blends the slowed down appeal of a ballad with the up-tempo (if only slightly) quality of her vocal tones on the chorus.<br /><br />`Forgotten' works, sort of, but it certainly tries a little too hard.  `Who Knows' is bouncy and interesting yet it kind of feels like fluff.  `Fall to Pieces' is soft and underwhelming.  `Freak Out' is cheap and the musical arrangement is near plagiarism, but there is some sense of fun in the chorus.<br /><br />But thankfully the closing track, `Slipped Away', is everything it needed to be in order to really wrap things up well.  Her vocals are strong, the chorus hits the right notes and the haunting quality she had been striving for on some of the other tracks comes full circle here and actually accomplishes what she wanted to.<br /><br />In the end I can't say that I hate this album, despite the fact that a good 50% or more of it is subpar and doesn't age well.  I just have this soft spot for Avril, and when she hits her highs she really soars.  This isn't the best effort from her (the second half of this album is a steep and steady free-fall) but it is still worth a listen, or two.l arrangement itself fees ripped straight from Green Day's `Insomniac' record.  `How Does It Feel' finds Avril trying to tap into the success of `I'm With You', slowing things down and trying to pull out a sincere and moving track that just doesn't do it. Sure, it isn't exactly the same breed of track, but it conveys the feelings that `How Does It Feel' couldn't.  Maybe that's because Avril vocals are better suited for `big' things.  She blends the slowed down appeal of a ballad with the up-tempo (if only slightly) quality of her vocal tones on the chorus.    `Forgotten' works, sort of, but it certainly tries a little too hard.  `Who Knows' is bouncy and interesting yet it kind of feels like fluff.  `Fall to Pieces' is soft and underwhelming.  `Freak Out' is cheap and the musical arrangement is near plagiarism, but there is some sense of fun in the chorus. It winds its way through six different stories that are interlocking - so it seems - first one way, then in reverse order.  A missionary on a less-than wholesome sea voyage, a British literary editor with high blood pressure, and a somewhat less-than-human fast-food worker are a few of the diverse denizens of these neighboring worlds. 1933771690|4.0|There has been a rather large volume of books in the past two or so decades dealing, one way or another, with the philosophical implications of the so-called New Physics (a term that refers mostly to Quantum Mechanics). The central thesis of most of these efforts has been, more or less, the idea that consciousness and the observer are not, shall we say, innocent bystanders in the great scheme of things, but actual participants. The level  of participation implied varies, with some authors allowing, often timidly, a certain degree of influence, while others boldly claim that without consciousness not only do we not have physics, but we do not actually have anything. Nothing at all. Robert Lanza and his book can be found in this latter category.    The book is well-written, with very easy-flowing language that is both easy to follow but also engaging. Lanza is not afraid to be humorous (when you reach the \\"George Clooney\\" comment you'll see what I mean), but also to include some personal details - that not only are not a digression, but, in a way, they are central to this narrative. At the same time, there is a very logical, coherent progression of his thesis. I think one of the most successful aspects of the book is that it should be enjoyable by readers of very different backgrounds. People who have never heard of quantum mechanics before, will not have any problem whatsoever following the arguments presented, as it's all explained, briefly and in a simple language - diagrams are also included. At the same time, people who have read about the topic before, will surely find new elements to stimulate their thought. Yes, all the \\"QM stuff\\" are there - double split, delayed choice, entanglement - but at no point did I feel bored or not the target audience.    So, what are the downsides of this book? What keeps it from being perfect? It is subtle, not easy to describe, but it is there. Perhaps in an attempt to underline the scientific scope of the book, Lanza treats philosophy as some kind of byproduct of thought that needs to be marginalized. It's not that he is too cold or emotionless - the exact opposite, rather; the text is full of emotion and passages from the works of such giants as Emerson and Thoreau. But, in my opinion, he could have ventured to be just a little more speculative. He does have a few pages near the end of the book in which some rather weak attempts at speculation are offered, but they are too meek.     In addition, I found two rather important omissions from a discourse on consciousness:  a) Ethics  b) Distinction between consciousness and identity    With regard to the former, I found it very puzzling and somewhat disappointing that ethics were not mentioned at all, and the relevant issue of free will is mentioned once, maybe twice, as fleeting and insignificant references. With regard to the latter, I got the impression that Lanza felt that such a task would be too daunting. But, alas, I was again left with a sense of incompleteness, for the notion of, shall we say, \\"My-consciousness-versus-your-consciousness\\" is crucial, and absolutely central. As far as I'm concerned, Amit Goswami's approach - in the style of \\"we don't 'have' consciousness; consciousness 'has' us\\" remains a pretty good starting point.    Having said that, I found Lanza's work extremely rewarding and thought-provoking, and I would warmly recommend it. It is truly a part of the paradigm-shift literature of our times.
067182449X|5.0|Under normal circumstances a diary written by a teen-age girl might not have much significance.  However, when the author writes her diary while hiding from oppressive forces occupying her country the diary has a much richer and lasting meaning.  Such is the case with Anne Frank: The Diary of a Young Girl.  Between 1942 and 1944 Anne kept a diary as her family and another family were hiding from the Nazis in Amsterdam.  Anne is very good at using description in her writing and utilizes the power of understatement quite effectively; she also is skilled at using short, crisp dialogue to liven up the various scenes she describes.  Despite her difficult circumstances she is able to maintain an optimistic outlook on life.  Though the reader can never quite shake the bitterness of knowing young Anne's life will end, there are pertinent and delightful coming of age themes explored throughout her writing; some of these themes include conflict with parents and young love.  Anne's writing definitely develops and matures during the course of the diary.  While this book calls the reader to not forget the Holocaust it also serves as a celebration of the vitality of youth.

At the time of writing this review, amazon is only offering copies through 'amazon re-sellers',  and the list price for a \\"new\\" copy is $90+, and for \\"used\\" it's $70+. Seems like someone is trying to artificially inflate the average price for these items, and hose-down the rest of us. These are not out-of-print or rare collectibles; again, someone in management seems asleep at the wheel. This item is still being printed and sold for less than a third of these prices everywhere else on the internet. You might say, \\"well, just go somewhere and buy the copy for less,\\" and I plan to, but not before I say my peace. I could be paying a reasonable price for one of my favorite films on Blu-Ray here at amazon, along with the rest of my order, but I'm being forced to go use other websites that I don't prefer. Hopefully, someone at amazon takes notice.     This Blu-Ray, and the movie itself both get 5 stars, but amazon gets abig fat ZERO. GG.

0316069884|3.0|This book in some ways was very hard for me to keep reading. Yet on the other hand, it was hard to put down. But, I did!  Foer's relentless, overly descriptive and repetitious point on \\"eating animals\\" begins to wear thin. While, I agree with some of Foer's points, his technique of \\"beating\\" the point to death becomes very un-inspiring after a while. Foer presents multiple points of view regarding his personal opinion on eating meat. His strength in this book, in my opinion, is his investigative journalism. His research and personal experiences are the most powerful and the most credible words in his message.    His relentless point: We as human beings need to be aware of the inhumane and the cruel treatment to which animals are subjected before they are ultimately a dish served to us for breakfast, lunch, dinner or even a measly snack.  Foer uses the unrealistic, but effective scenario, at the beginning of his book when he talks about eating man's best friend, our family pet, the dog. This is the big bang at the beginning in which Foer powers home his message and his opinion on eating animals. I thought this was an effective strategy for Foer, because it captured the attention of the reader quickly! However, after the first chapter Foer had made his point and my interest slowly diminished.     This book has some interesting dimensions which force the reader to think about what they eat and where it comes from. On the other hand, in his quest to change America's eating habits, Foer goes a little over the top by ramming his views and opinions down the reader's throat. It is all or nothing for Foer when it comes to eating any food that is not grown in the ground. There is no middle ground. What are we to do, if there were massive crop failures? Furthermore, it is not even acceptable, in Foer's point of view, to eat meat that comes from humane family farms vs. inhumane factory farms. Eggs are another bone of contention, because of the way chickens are treated. We weremade aware of his opinion on eating animals, once again, by his relentless descriptive words regarding chicken cages, chicken farms and more.    I have mixed feelings about this book. While there are interesting and factual aspects within the book, it seems to me that Foer is more interested in forcing his own personal feelings and his moral agenda on his readers.
0765356139|3.0|I like the first book in the series, Mistborn. I hadn't read any of Sanderson's original works (is Elantris the only one?) prior to my Mistborn experience, but I think that reading him as he finishes Robert Jordan's Wheel of Time series made me expect a bit more from his Mistborn works.    Don't get me wrong - I love the concept of Allomancy and how Sanderson marries that with the typical fantasy storyline of Evil Oppressor and Powerful Bad Magic. I just thought he needed to develop characters more evenly and give his world a more vibrant feel.     The Well of Ascension is a step forward along those lines.     I started to feel more depth to the overall story in this book. Sanderson is developing characters and peeking in on things we only guessed at in the first novel. One - the dialog seems a little forced and doesn't flow naturally within the story at times. That could use some fine-tuning. Two - I always like it when authors tell you up front to read What Has Come Before; in other words, don't start reading Book 2 without reading Book 1. This would save all the \\"catch up\\" text in subsequent novels that tire constant readers of the series.     If you liked Mistborn and thought the series had promise, The Well of Ascension is a good read and I recommend it. If you're expecting Jordan-esque depth to the story, you may be a bit disappointed.

When lives come together and blur the lines between the past and the present. When truths inevitably come out stirring lives. After being separated for three years, Adam and Mia have taken completely different paths. But seeing each other again and being together for one day, changes that. The story is told from Adam's pov, and turns out that Gayle Forman managed to do Adam's voice so amazingly well done, that I sort of forgot all my former empathy for Mia, and dove into Adam's mind, feeling his hurt and his attempts to hide them as if they were my own. Everything stirred up more feelings, more words than the ones that were written. I felt a whirlwind inside of me. And darn, that is something very hard to achieve in an avid reader. This book with its all-over depth and gut-wrenching emotional baggage, completely surpassed my love for If I Stay. This story ruined all other love stories for me. And ruined me.    These feelings, this book, this story... they will always haunt me. These feelings linger just like our own feelings. The tension was so palpable and the story was so memorable that I doubt I will ever read such a majestic book ever again. I have my hopes, though.    Given the fact that no matter what I say, it will never be enough nor will it ever do the book justice, I think YOU should read this.
B000T9QF8S|5.0|This is America, and I love lively discussions, but trashing one of the finest Beethoven cycles out in the history of recorded sound and the man with the most Grammy awards (30+) is just plain wrong.  I am a musician, and spend over 20 hours a week on the average listening to, writing, or engineering recordings.  I don't  play at being an armchair musicologist, but please allow me make a few observations and recommendations.<br /><br />First, let me address sound and technical engineering.  Until the recent recordings and SACD technology, these recordings had more clarity and \\"bite\\" than just about anything else out there.  You feel the brass, you can hear the bow hair hitting the strings, and the mallets on the tympani.  All without feeling the mikes are so close you and the orchestra are in a bathtub like on some of the original instrument cycles.  Listen for yourself.  Go to where you would download the mp3s and listen to the samples.  You get a sense of spaciousness without the overwhelming reverb, precision, and then there is the performance.  Solti's takes a traditional big orchestra approach with tempo's that aren't as brisk (and to my ears, rushed) as some of the original instrument recordings.  What Solti delivers is power and excitement that is as visual as any painting in the Louvre.<br /><br />The high points in this cycle are the entire Fifth Symphony, the Sixth, the Seventh, and the second and fourth movements of Symphony # 9.  In fact, I would go as far to say that Solti's 1970s version of the Ninth is one of the greatest recordings in history.  Martti Talvela's entrance in the 4th movement will send chills down your spine if you have the right stereo set up for it.  If there was ever an good excuse to blast the neighborhood to Jupiter, this would be it.  The other soloists and chorus are spot and turn this piece into an event...something to experience, and often.  I have to admit I like Norrington's version (with the London Classical Players on EMI) of the First and Second Symphonies [[ASIN:B00005A9O0 Beethoven - Symphonies 1-9  Overtures / London Classical Players  Sir Roger Norrington]].  Some critics and a lot of folks hate those.  These two works were a little more like what Haydn would have turn out and Norrington's approach works for those pieces.  And as good as these recordings are, I still have a soft spot the Bruno Walter's recordings of the 4th and 5th Symphonies with the Columbia Symphony [[ASIN:B0000027UK Beethoven: The Complete Symphonies]].<br /><br />Is this version the best?  Goodness, who's to say?  In my opinion, you may get different, but you aren't going to get better.  Comparing versions of recorded classical works is like comparing apples, oranges, and motorcycles.  Toscanini's version with the NBC Symphony has lyricism and a fine sense of feel for melody.  It's also in mono.  Worth owning, but it's early days of mono.  Karajan's cycle is astounding, especially the SACD version.  The Minnesota Orchestra just turned out one of the most critically acclaimed cycles in years, and I highly recommend giving that a spin [[ASIN:B002QEXN6Q Beethoven: The Symphonies]] .  Classical music lovers tend to like the version they hear first and then judge other versions against that one.<br /><br />Give this a listen.  99 per cent of you won't be disappointed.  Highly recommended.of the First and Second Symphonies [[ASIN:B00005A9O0 Beethoven - Symphonies 1-9  Overtures / London Classical Players  Sir Roger Norrington]]. I did not read the print version. In 13+ hours Frank McCourt reads his own book. In 13 hours we hear/see/feel and know dire poverty, a longing to belong to another or better class of poverty. We hear the pain in the young boy's voice as he tries to make sense of his wayward, loving, funny, supportive father. We hear the pain of the man-child recognizing his mother's desperate straits.<br /><br />We learn about all the players in this comedy he called his childhood. All the while, the big pink elephant dwells in the family's midst, some side-stepping it, some succumbing to it.<br /><br />We suffer for his mother, for his lost siblings, for the loss of a real father. We rejoice in young Frances' accomplishments -- stealing, lying, and generally getting over -- just to survive.<br /><br />Yet the main theme of this tremendous treatise on dysfunctionality is that of Frank's father, Malachy. We love him, hate him, pity him, loathe him and try to understand why Angela tolerates it all. And through young Frankie's burning, sick eyes, we see a desperation that cannot go away.<br /><br />The children grow up not questioning their lot in life, though Frankie's thoughts are ever clear and demanding. Somehow through all the neglect and borderline abuse that Frankie survives, he is polite, kind and knows his place.<br /><br />He is a true Catholic, reared in the old ways of repentence, shame and forgiveness. He can sin and when he is able to, confess and then be redeemed, knowing he will slide again and repeat the cycle.<br /><br />The peripheral characters in his story are as strong and shaping as his absent father and unconscious mother. They shape him. Alcoholism abounds in his world and it too shapes him.. without saying it, it is the omnipotent pint that shapes his views, his mores, his goals.<br /><br />Frank stumbles into adolescence wiht a bang. He is ill, his eyes are horrific, but his sight is clear, his voice is strong and his will to \\"go to America\\" prevails.<br /><br />America represents his infancy, when there was hope in Angela's heart, when Malachy reluctantly claimed Frankie as his son, yet lived his \\"Northern Irish\\" life as if Frankie was not there.<br /><br />America beckons young Frances whose real first name is NOT Frances, because life in Ireland was so, so bad. During his years between 14 and 19 he longed for and saved for America, knowing America would change his life. He was a minor criminal, far beyond stealing to survive. He was able to save money and eat and do all sorts of things separate from his hungry mother and siblings and felt no remorse.<br /><br />Already he was becoming Malachy's offspring. Frankie would do whatever he had todo or wanted to do.. to pursue his dream --- in this case, America.<br /><br />Frank's father Malachy wanted only enough money to drink and declare his love of country. Malachy never wanted a family; his ready made family came as a result of a momentary dalliance with young Angela in America. Though they had six children, Malachy never accepted his role as father or provider.<br /><br />Angela reluctantly took on her role as bearer of children, but not one time, not once in the entire book did I hear the words love from either parent or any child.<br /><br />This was a life of obligation. Children were born, children died. Sometimes they ate, sometimes they starved. No matter how little money or food (the babies had sugar water instead of milk), Malachy Sr. had his pint(s) of Guiness.<br /><br />While Frank studies his childhood with a poignant view of his pains and privations, he has warm, loving memories of moments with each of his family members. He loves the time in front of the cold fire place with his father, who is fatherly at those morning meetings. There are the moments on the stairs of their first apartment, the long walks in the country stealing apples and milk, all those wonderful things he recalls.<br /><br />But Frank's eyes look for America as his salvation. Ironically, his eyes are his major physical weakness. We wonder at the end... what about his eyes?<br /><br />Frank does get to America and is welcomed by a great event that proves right off the boat that America is the land of opportunity.<br /><br />In summary, it is a long, longing and profound look at a life, a country, a time of life and a perspective that rings true and truly harrowing all at the same time.<br /><br />I came to cheer Frankie on in his exploits, while wincing in discomfort as I realize that these are the traits he will take with him into adulthood and to America. How honest will Frank be in his lifetime, will he become an American criminal too?<br /><br />These are the elements that make for good stories, strong characters that create a \\"need to know\\" in the reader.<br /><br />Throughout the book I came to realize that had I read this book when I was a young parent, my children would have turned out differently today. I would have been more restricting, given them less and taught them more. Frank's family got by on virtually nothing -- and nothing within a community of little more than nothing. Someone described one of their apartments as resembling Calcutta.<br /><br />They were poorer than dirt and they had no way out.<br /><br />Read this story with joy and anticipation. The pay-off is slow and agonizing, but in retrospect it is there.\\"<br /><br />Patricia G. Kurz, Resident Scholar<br /><br />A great all time classic and touching memoir.<br /><br />5/5 stars.y. Though they had six children, Malachy never accepted his role as father or provider. B004289ZW0|2.0|I don't have the swipe-on/swipe-off problem other folks have in this forum.  That feature seems to work fine.  What I am disappointed in is its basic specs.  I should have been a bit more suspicious when basic stats such as accuracy and refresh rate are impossible to find on the GNS web site.  Upon receiving the unit, I can confirm the following:    It uses an older version MediaTek chipset.  As a result, its accuracy is no better than the internal GPS on the iPhone 4 and iPad 2.  In fact, in general, if the GNS 5870 is showing a horizontal accuracy of 10 meters and a vertical accuracy of 10 meters (10/10), then the internal GPS of the iPhone 4 will typically show 5/10.  iPad 2 was a little closer, sometimes beating the GNS, most of the time matching it.  This was tested using GPS-Status app from the app store.  Its refresh rate is only 1hz and is not configurable.      I would have expected a unit such as this to have much better accuracy than the internal chipsets, and for it to not have at least a 5 hz refresh rate is pretty lame.    On the plus side, its Bluetooth pairing is quite fast and reliable.  As a result, I could recommend this product as a backfill for non-GPS iPods and iPads, as well as older GPS-enabled models.  I cannot recommend it as a more accurate replacement for internal chipsets on the latest models of iPhones and iPads.

0061726826|5.0|I started reading Delirium with some trepidation.  One, I tend to be picky when it comes to YA, and two, the hype for this book has been out of this world. Delirium takes place in a present day, alternate Portland, and the President and the Consortium has declared love to be a disease (amor delirium nervosa). The declaration was made 64 years ago, and a \\"cure\\" was discovered 43 years ago. Everyone aged 18 is given the cure, and that's where we find our narrator, 17 year old Lena, on the cusp of her 18th birthday, and the promise of a \\"better\\" future. A future without love.<br /><br />In Lena's world, everything is assigned; schools, majors...mates. Romeo and Juliet is required reading...as a cautionary tale. There is actually an emergency toll-free line that can be called if someone is suspected of contacting deliria. Regulators, groups of volunteer citizens and government employees, patrol the streets at night, looking for \\"uncureds\\" out after curfew. Without love, some parents find parenting distasteful, often killing their children, and the government constantly monitors communications, looking for keywords such as \\"love\\", \\"Invalids\\", or \\"sympathizer\\". Invalids are sympathizers that live in the Wilds, the unregulated land that exists between recognized cities and towns. The borders of the US were closed 50 years ago and are now guarded constantly by military personnel. Sympathizers are summarily executed.<br />When a sympathizer group interrupts Lena's exams, which will decide what her future will hold, she gets her first glimpse of Alex, and when they finally meet, Lena will question everything that she's been taught, because Alex has a secret.<br /><br />Lena is awkward and unsure of herself, describing herself as neither pretty nor ugly, and in her words, just short. She especially feels plain next to her best friend Hana, who is beautiful, and confident; everything Lena feels she is not. Lauren Oliver's prose is smooth, lyrical, and sometimes brutal.  We are drawn into Lena's world of fear; fear of deliria, and also fear of being \\"cured\\". As Lena struggles to cope with her family's legacy, her war within herself, and her ultimate awakening, we are reminded of what it means to love, and be loved, how without pain, love would be meaningless, and how fragile we really are. Delirium took me back and made me remember what it was like to fall in love for the first time, to feel like I was I was drifting in a sparkling, clear blue sea, with no fear of drowning, and no wish to come up for air. When a revelation about Lena's past is uncovered, she will question everything that she has ever known, about her mother, about herself, and make a decision that will change everything.<br /><br />There were parts of this novel that chilled me, scenes that struck me to my core, sometimes moved me to tears, and made me realize how lucky we are to have choices; choices in life, education, friendships, and, perhaps most importantly, choices in love. The only thing missing, for me, is the big why of how love came to be thought of as a disease. We're given the obvious reasons: pay special attention to the \\"quotes\\" at the beginning of each chapter from publications like \\"The Safety, Health, and Happiness Handbook\\", better known as The Book of Shhh (some are fairly disturbing), and Genesis: A Complete History of the World and the Known Universe. However, I'd like to know what would make people want to take away the one thing that really defines us as human beings, and sometimes gives us a reason to live in even the darkest times. Maybe in a future novel?<br /><br />With the recent influx of dystopian YA fiction, Delirium is a standout, not to be missed!rld of fear; fear of deliria, and also fear of being \\"cured\\". The Victorians were fascinated by the allegorical drama illustrated in The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde.  The book was so popular, it inspired stage plays a year after its publications. 078322947X|3.0|Sorcerer works most effectively for passionate students of film. It's interesting to see a more modern take on the Wages of Fear story, and it works on a meta level as a quasi-Apocalypse Now/Fitzcarraldo bit of cursed ambitious-production filmmaking.    Unlike those films, however, Sorcerer seems to have been crippled by focus on stunts and production, over plot and pacing. The original Wages of Fear lagged needlessly in the first hour, and Sorcerer only extends that part of the story to no particular purpose besides a sort of self-indulgent wallowing. Extra time is spent on giving additional background to the protagonists, but we get to know them in less depth psychologically as individuals than in the original.    The framing of shots is painfully dated, distinctly 80s in style. The synthesizer score, praised in some quarters, only adds to the limited 80s vibe of the piece. It's never a good sign when the 50s version feels more modern than the more recent remake.    Roy Scheider is as good as ever, which is to say excellent. Unfortunately, the editing gives little visibility to the best aspects of his performance potential. Bruno Cremer brings little to the table. The other leads are forgettable/inadvertently comical.    I would recommend purchasing and viewing the movie, but read up on its production history first [...]. It'll make the stodgy pacing more bearable, and allow you to enjoy the stunt set pieces in their full, blinkered glory.

B004LAIU4I|5.0|Paul Simon continues to follow his own path, creating concept albums that are as unique and creative as anything you will hear in pop music today. With \\"So Beautiful Or So What\\" he as weaved together a gorgeously colorful cloth of musical colors and all 9 songs (including one short instrumental) stitch together to make a musical and lyrical tapestry that rivals his masterful \\"Graceland\\" and \\"Rhythm Of The Saints\\". The opening number is the hook that grabs the listener and doesn't let go until the final, triumphant song. \\"Getting Ready For Christmas Day\\" starts the album off with a bang, a rhythmic riff on mortality that duets with an early 1940's recording of a Gospel sermon by Reverend J.M. Gates. This is a stunning example of recording art, where Simon's lyrics, his musicians and an old audio tape converge to  make a classic Simon song ; and it only gets better after that! \\"The Afterlife', \\"Rewrite\\",\\"\\"So Beautiful or So What\\" continue the lovely trend of percussion melded with witty, often oddly moving lyrics that marked the genius of \\"Graceland and \\" Rhythm Of The Saints\\". But there are softer sounds here, songs that are contemplative, somewhat mournful tunes but flecked with hope. \\"Love And Hard Times\\" and \\"Questions For The Angels\\" are reminiscent to a degree of Classic Simon ballads of the past, but understandably more mature in outlook. Frankly, writing a review or, in this case, a comment on this album seems rather futile because the reward is in the listening. Simon has remarked that this new work contains songs connected by a thread, hence his referring to \\"So Beautiful or So What\\" as a true concept album. As far as the current recording industry and the average listener is concerned, this enthralling record (forgive my anachronistic term) could possibly fall on deaf ears. But Paul Simon continues to have a fiercely loyal fan base , made of of what I suspect are baby boomers of a certain age, like Simon himself) who should get many hours of enjoyment along with some serious reflection listening to this gorgeous album. One final thought: \\"Love Is Eternal Sacred Light\\" is as propulsive and intoxicating a number as he has ever produced. Who cares if this album is denied Grammy Nominations (considering the sad state of affairs regarding that rewarding organization) : this is a flat out masterpiece.<br /><br />IN RESPONSE TO SOME READERS' REVIEWS WHO FIND THAT PAUL SIMON IS RAMMING HIS RELIGIOUS/SPIRITUAL VIEWS DOWN THEIR THROATS :<br /><br />Paul Simon has stated that he is \\"not religious\\". I have no idea what Mr. Simon's views on religion are,  his religious beliefs, etc. However, aside from the obvious fact that he writes compassionately regarding the human condition, I proffer that his use of religious imagery (angels, heaven, \\"the afterlife\\", eternal, sacred, light, celestial ladders : all that  emanates a great deal from his literary background. Simon was an English Major in College and it shows in countless number of songs he has written in the past 50 years. \\"Bleecker Street' is a prime example, where \\"holy\\" and \\"sacrament' are intoned by the beautifully blended voices of Simon and Garfunkle on the Wednesday Morning 3 A.M. album. Then there is \\"Bridge Over Troubled Water\\", one of the great pop songs of the twentieth century, a song inspired by the great gospel singers earlier in the century and one song in particular, \\"Mary, Don't You Weep\\". Simon has worked closely and been influenced by gospel groups and the gospel \\"sound' is something that seems to energize his creativity in writing songs. On \\"Surprise\\" his previous work before \\"So Beautiful...\\" Simon movingly conjures up a surreal image of light passing through a church's stained glass window :<br /><br />  \\"And the frayed cuffs and collars were mended by haloes of golden thread. The choir sang, \\"Once Upon A Time There Was An Ocean\\". And all the old hymns and family names came fluttering down as leaves of emotion\\".<br /><br />Finally, there is one of the greatest songs he has ever written, on the \\"You're the One\\" album (2000). It is entitled \\"Love\\" and the song's final words are as apocalyptic, haunting and beautiful as anything he has written :<br /><br />  \\"THE PRICE THAT WE PAY<br />  WHEN EVIL WALKS THE PLANET<br />  AND LOVE IS CRUSHED LIKE CLAY<br />  THE MASTER RACES, THE CHOSEN PEOPLES, THE BURNING TEMPLES, THE WEEPING CATHEDRALS.\\"er written, on the \\"You're the One\\" album (2000). B004LWZVZC|5.0|Most American's are familiar with the Civil War and its aftermath, but one of the lesser known aspects of the Civil War is the rounding up of conspirators in the assassination of Abraham Lincoln and their subsequent trial, in particular the case of Mary Surratt. This is the story Robert Redford brings us in \\"The Conspirator.\\"<br /><br />The story in \\"The Conspirator\\" isn't one we're all unfamiliar with. A young inexperienced lawyer, Frederick Aiken (James McAvoy) is assigned a client he initially thinks is guilty, Mary Suratt (Robin Wright) in a conspiracy to assassinate Abraham Lincoln. As facts come to light and the prosecution obviously takes liberties in trying to hide evidence, pressure witnesses, and allow for only one viewpoint of evidence, that he slowly comes to the realization that while his client maybe guilty she isn't getting a fair trial and provides a more vigorous defense then at first thought possible. John Grisham has told this story many times.<br /><br />The movie is told from Aiken's point of view in his defense of Suratt, how it affects his life and relationships, and as his defense becomes more effective how he's exiled from Washington society and he starts to become suspect by his friends and the very people that appointed him to the position. In brief flashbacks we're also shown just enough of the conspiracy and the action of the conspirators that we can know what's going on.<br /><br />This isn't a glamor role for Robin Wright she barely wears make up, her lips dry and parched after being in prison for a month. She plays Suratt with matronly dignity expected of a woman of the age. James McAvoy gives a strong performance as a Union Captain who comes back from war and wants to return to his private life only to be thrust back into fighting, only this time in the courtroom. Kevin Kline's Edwin Stanton is absolutely ruthless in his pursuit of the assassins of Abraham Lincoln, and we're shown that ruthlessness almost immediately when he bars the distraught Mary Todd Lincoln from the President's side after he's taken from Ford's theater. In the supporting cast Evan Rachael Wood as Anna Surrat stands out, and Stephen Root, while having a small role always makes it interesting.<br /><br />Director Robert Redford shot this film beautifully, the gaslights glisten, and a couple of times looks like a Georges Seurat painting, perhaps in contrast to the cramped and war torn buildings most of the action occurs in and around.<br /><br />Yes, Redford's story has resonances and similarities to today's world, but history always will be relevant to the day, and the adage that \\"those who cannot remember the past are condemned to repeat it\\" was never truer. \\"The Conspirator\\" reminds us of that history. But we aren't beaten over the head with a \\"message,\\" Redford gives voice to both sides of the argument, with Stanton arguing that all measures should be taken to roundup the conspirators and put in front of a military tribunal in the name of expediency in healing a nation torn apart. Aiken voices the other side of the argument, of why do we fight wars in the name of freedom and liberty if we're just to put them to the side? Although, Redford leans to the side of when the world changes the constitution shouldn't.<br /><br />I've read a bit about the Civil War, and thought Mary Suratt, while railroaded by Stanton, had known what her son and Booth were up to. B0048EANOW|5.0|This was the best film made in '97 but like \\"Chinatown,\\" another noir, it is destined to become one of the finest movies not to take the Oscar home. Curtis Hanson took James Ellroy's novel, a book many doubted could be translated to the film medium, and crafted one of the finest adapted screenplays ever written. He then brought on board a couple of Aussie unknowns, a gorgeous star who had never lived up to her potential, and one of the finest actors working in film today. He then began filming one of the darkest noir films of all time in the blinding L.A. sun.<br /><br />The end result is a dark and twisted tale of personal redemption told against the backdrop of the bright lights and sunshine of Hollywood in the early 1950's. Hanson contrasted the brightly lit exteriors with Ellroy's dark storyline of police corruption and Hollywood decadence. This is a movie about facade, not just Hollywood's but our own personal front as well.<br /><br />Russell Crowe became a star as Detective Bud White, a tough cop willing to do whatever was necessary, which was in stark contrast to his politically minded colleague Guy Pearce. He finds Bud archaic, representing everything about the force he wants to change. What may stop him from doing so is his investigation into the murder of several people at The Night Owl cafe, one of whom is Crowe's partner, kicked off the force after a well publicized jail brawl christened given the handle \\"Bloody Christmas\\" by the papers.<br /><br />Crowe and Pearce come at it from different angles but the road for both leads right to beautiful Kim Basinger and a millionaire in the lush Hollywood hills played by David Strathairn. There is a reason Basinger looks a little like Veronica Lake the first time we see her in this film; she's supposed to. Hollywood legend has it that a string of expensive call girls were cut to look like stars during the 1940's and early 1950's and Hanson has made this darker side of Hollywood part of the story. Basinger is one of the lucky ones; close enough to the actual look of Veronica Lake not to have been cut on.<br /><br />Crowe falls for the real girl inside Basinger, but in spite of her opulent lifestyle, her low self-esteem comes to the forefront when she sleeps with Pearce in an attempt to \\"help\\" Crowe. We realize as she nearly destroys Crowe by doing so that she perceives herself as a whore on the inside, beneath the facade. Her logic is as twisted and tainted as the corruption Crowe and Pierce are about to uncover as they follow the trail linking Basinger's boss Strathairn to the Night Owl killings and the vice surrounding them on every side.<br /><br />Basinger may have garnered the Oscar for her role, but Crowe's performance as the tough cop with some soft spots after all is something you'll always remember. The coolest job done here, however, is by Kevin Spacey. Hanson told him before filming began to think Dean Martin and he'd have it down pat. Spacey did just that, playing the ultra cool cop, the one in the tabloids for his Hollywood connections. He is a consultant on the TV show \\"Badge of Honor\\" (think Dragnet) and is hooked up with slimy but likable Danny DeVito, a writer for a Hollywood tabloid. Spacey grabs the spotlight and DeVito gets the headlines as Spacy collars Hollywood stars in compromising situations, DeVito's camera flashing.<br /><br />Spacey seemingly has it all, but like the rest of this film, it is just a facade. While sitting in a bar listening to Dean Martin in the background he looks up into the mirror behind the counter and doesn't like what is staring back at him. He has all the tools to be a great cop but he knows he has sold his soul for the fifty in front of him. He becomes involved in the case because of a murder in a hotel room he feels responsible for that leads right back to the Night Owl, and hooks up with Pearce to redeem his soul. You will never forget the name \\"Rollo Tomasi\\" or what it means for Pearce, and ultimately Spacey in this film.<br /><br />Adding to the atmosphere more than just a little is the score by Jerry Goldsmith, his finest work since \\"Chinatown\\" and just as haunting. It does more than help enhance the atmosphere, it truly is the atmosphere of this one of a kind masterpiece. This film has the kind of ending dreams are made of and someone holding up their badge to the oncoming rush of cop cars in the Hollywood hills at night is a scene you'll never forget.<br /><br />There is not a bad performance in this film. It is complex and riveting, and one you'll watch it over and over. 0374158460|4.0|At this point (some 800+ reviews now posted on Amazon) and with all the praise that has been showered on \\"Freedom\\" from the New York Times to the New Yorker (any surprise they both loved it?), what I can offer is my reaction to reading this mountain of a book.<br />For me, it was an enjoyable slog (if that's possible).<br /><br />And I felt manipulated at the same time.  I felt like my face was being shoved into these people's lives. Franzen allows no distance.  You live with the bodily fluids of his characters and, in one case, with even more. There are feces smeared on the page (almost literally). Franzen forces his characters to handle them and, by extension, we are right there too, our noses down in the waste and the muck.<br /><br />And, at the same time, I listened on audio CD and found myself easily pulled along. There's internal and external conflict in abundance.<br />There are big themes, big ideas and three-dimensional characters with a whole big range of human emotions.  If Franzen set out to capture the zeitgeist of the Modern White Middle Class American, I'd say he succeeded in a Very Big Franzen way.<br /><br />But there are also Big Soap Opera moments.  No matter all the background and build-up, you can't overlook the clich&eacute; climactic fight between Walter and Patty.  \\"For God's sake, though, Walter, it was just sex,\\" she asserts mid-battle.  Of course we know it was much more than \\"just sex\\" because we know every morsel of her soul by this point (page 462). We know the all the self-destructive forces of all the characters because Franzen won't let us overlook them.<br /><br />He tells us everything.  There is never a moment when you wonder what these characters think.<br />\\"Feeling cruelly deprived, she turned off the television.\\"<br /><br />\\"Walter felt, himself, is his anger and disappointment with the world, like the gray northern woods.\\"<br /><br />\\"By finally venting his anger, first to Patty and then in Whitmanville, and thereby extricating himself from his marriage and from the Trust, he'd removed two major causes of his anger.\\"<br /><br />Writing schools tell you to \\"show don't tell.\\"<br /><br />Franzen's characters have emotions and feelings that have grown muscles. Then, he puts them in situations where they must bench-press 400 pounds or clean-and-lift 250.  Even in the case of hard-to-like Walter Berglund, who can't even be honest with himself about his own needs, his self-loathing is a powerful, well-chiseled force.<br /><br />In fact, the characters are hard to like but the story pulls you along.  There's a ton of sex--or yearning for sex.<br /><br />At the same time, there's too much back story in spots (for my taste); did we need to know, four-fifths of the way through, about Walter's father and his Swedish upbringing? Another example of Franzen's in-your-face power.  He's fearless, alright.<br /><br />OK, I'll say this: \\"Freedom\\" is unforgettable.  For me, it's just a bit of a mess and I wish someone could have had the guts to give it a trim.<br /><br />I'm really somewhere between three and four stars but on Amazon I see the one-star reviews are now outnumbering the five-star.<br /><br />So, Franzen must need some love.<br /><br />Here it is.e'd removed two major causes of his anger.\\"    Writing schools tell you to \\"show don't tell.\\"    Franzen's characters have emotions and feelings that have grown muscles. 0756404738|4.0|Creating an epic fantasy protagonist is like answering job-interview questions: the trick is to create flaws that are not-so-secretly strengths, adding quirks and idiosyncrasies that make one's hero more impressive rather than less. Patrick Rothfuss has passed a Google-level interview with his trilogy's hero Kvothe, a young hothead with a list of accomplishments and abilities as long as his arm but also a carefully calibrated set of disadvantages (very young, redheaded, from the Gypsy-esque traveling folk of this continent, poor, orphaned) that serve primarily to make him even more impressive to the reader.<br /><br />Rothfuss introduced Kvothe in [[ASIN:0756405890 The Name of the Wind]] four years ago, which immediately grabbed him a large and appreciative audience -- and no wonder, since it was a compellingly readable coming-of-age story with an iconic first-person narrator and a frame story that dribbled hints of things to come (both triumphs and tragedies) in the way that prophecy-loving fantasy readers immediately jumped on. NAME OF THE WIND was also a big book, full of exciting scenes, and secondary-world fantasy readers seem to still value size over everything -- size of book, size of protagonist's ego and abilities, size of scale, size of anything that can be measured. So Rothfuss had an almost immediate fanbase, which, as always, wanted to know how quickly they could expect the next book.<br /><br />It took longer than expected, but THE WISE MAN'S FEAR hit stores last month with an even larger thump than NAME OF THE WIND did. (The new book is just shy of a thousand pages, and those pages have substantial numbers of words on them -- again, size is a huge bonus in this market.) And it's very much the same kind of book as NAME OF THE WIND was -- a little bit of frame story, in which an older Kvothe is seen, reduced to sad and unlikely circumstances, telling the tale of his much younger days, and in which Rothfuss throws out as many as a dozen dark hints a page as narrative anchors for the stuff he'll write later or, perhaps, leave as hints if he doesn't manage to get to them; and then a whole lot of \\"when I was young\\" stuff. In the main text, Kvothe only ages about a year -- he's still in his mid-teens, and one begins to wonder if this series will burst beyond its promised trilogy (as so many others have), or if that last book will appear in twenty years as a 10,000-page e-only monster. In keeping with Rothfuss's teasing narrative strategy, most of the events the reader is anticipating happen later in Fear than expected -- if they happen at all. (See elsewhere on the Internet for the blow-by-blow details of those; like most popular secondary-world fantasy, there are now multiples of words written about this series than there are in the books themselves.)<br /><br />Rothfuss has an admirable control of scene and tension for a guy only on his second novel; this is a very big book with an overgrown \\"and\\" structure (\\"first this happened, and then this happened, and then...\\"), but it feels structured and moves at a controlled pace at all times. It is huge, though, meaning that all readers will not view all parts of it as equally necessary, and some sophisticated readers may begin to wonder if Rothfuss is just going to keep tossing out hints and not bothering to follow up on them. The frame story is more intrusive in this volume as well, and more obviously counterpointing Kvothe's stories about his youth. (Though there's still no evidence at all that Rothfuss wants us to doubt a word Kvothe says; he's a deeply reliable narrator, as usual in this genre.)<br /><br />In the end, this series -- Rothfuss has grandly titled it \\"The Kingkiller Chronicles,\\" based on yet another exploit of Kvothe's that we have gotten only the faintest reports of so far -- is a strong entry in the traditional Tolkeinesque secondary-world fantasy genre, but doesn't transcend that genre. Kvothe is an engaging narrator -- I almost wrote \\"endlessly engaging,\\" but that might be too much of a temptation to the already-given-to-great-length Rothfuss -- and his story is one of the better epic fantasies that you could find. But it doesn't push the genre in ways that Steven Erikson or George R.R. Martin do; it just settles into the middle of the territory to tell the story it has.f a temptation to the already-given-to-great-length Rothfuss -- and his story is one of the better epic fantasies that you could find. But it doesn't push the genre in ways that Steven Erikson or George R.R. Martin do; it just settles into the middle of the territory to tell the story it has.

And we're also right at the point where the '70s turned into the '80s, with the gleeful anarchy of the National Lampoon years ([[ASIN:B0000A02TZ Animal House]], [[ASIN:B000OMD3K8 Meatballs]], etc.) starting to turn into the crisp-and-slick sheen of the Reagan years. A bunch of odd teams -- Dean Martin and Sammy Davis, Jr. dressed up as priests in a Ferrari, Mel Tillis and Terry Bradshaw (and a half-ton of Budweiser) in a painted-over NASCAR vehicle, two guys on a motorcycle, Jamie Farr as a random Arab sheik, and so on -- compete for glory and a big check, by driving as quickly as possible across the country. Our hero is Burt, of course, and his sidekick is Dom DeLuise. They're driving an ambulance, so, for reasons never quite stated by obvious enough for a comedy like this, they need both a doctor and a patient. The former is Jack Elam, in one of his late bug-eyed comedy roles, and the latter is Farrah Fawcett, in a tight shirt and no bra.<br /><br />A series of gags and car-chase scenes follow, though very little that could be described as a single, coherent plot. But who wanted that, anyway? Eventually, everyone makes it to California and the end of the race. Again, this is a silly comedy, very much of its time -- but that was a fun, easy-going, big-smile kind of time in America, and there's nothing at all wrong with that.gags and car-chase scenes follow, though very little that could be described as a single, coherent plot.
